a. Scarcity Modeling
The system must emulate the natural scarcity of comics as accurately as possible:
1. Census-Based Scarcity:
o Use real-world CGC, CBCS, PGX, and auction data to define the total available
supply for each comic at app launch.
o Hard caps reflect total supply forever (e.g., Action Comics #1 at 100 copies
globally).
2. Dynamic Scarcity:
o Player Activity: As players buy, hold, or sell comics, the available supply
decreases.
▪ Example: If 40 out of 100 Action Comics #1 copies are in player
portfolios, only 60 are available for trade.
o News Events: New announcements or media impacts can increase demand,
driving down effective supply.
▪ Example: A Wonder Woman movie announcement boosts demand for
Sensation Comics #1, driving up scarcity.
3. Scarcity Multiplier:
o Scarcity influences value directly:
Scarcity Multiplier=Initial Census−Locked SupplyInitial Census\text{Scarcity
Multiplier} = \frac{\text{Initial Census} - \text{Locked Supply}}{\text{Initial
Census}}Scarcity Multiplier=Initial CensusInitial Census−Locked Supply
o The smaller the available supply, the higher the multiplier applied to the comic’s
base price.

o Example: A sudden increase in scarcity for Hulk #181 (Wolverine’s first
appearance) after a news announcement.
2. Liquidity:
o Define how easily a comic can be traded:
▪ High-value comics (Action Comics #1) have low liquidity.
▪ Lower-value comics (Amazing Spider-Man #300) are more liquid.
3. Risk and Opportunity Cost:
o Owning rare, expensive comics carries the risk of slow growth.
o Mid-tier comics may offer better short-term returns but less stability.
4. Comparison to Other Asset Classes:
o Tie comic market performance to external indices (e.g., Yahoo Finance Comics
Index).
o Compare returns to traditional assets like stocks or gold.

o Example: Scarcity is based on initial census and locked supply.
o Justify weighting of metrics like cultural impact, scarcity, and affordability.
2. Key Formulas:
o Document all calculations:
▪ Scarcity Multiplier.
▪ Weighted price changes based on news or events.
▪ Portfolio performance metrics.
3. Testing and Iteration:
o Simulate various scenarios to test market dynamics:
▪ Example: What happens to Sensation Comics #1 when 50% of its supply
is locked?
o Track outcomes to refine the system.
4. Change Log:
o Record all adjustments and their rationale:
▪ Example: If scarcity weighting proves too volatile, document why and
how it’s adjusted.

1. Scarcity Model Design
The scarcity model calculates and updates the availability of each comic (or creator card) daily.
This number becomes the basis for all value changes and portfolio dynamics.

o Derived from CGC, CBCS, PGX census data, and auction house records.

o Player Activity:
▪ Locked Supply: Comics in player portfolios are "locked" and unavailable
for trade.
▪ Market Liquidity: Comics bought/sold on the market adjust availability.
o News Events:
▪ Positive/negative news increases or decreases demand, affecting scarcity.
o Attrition:
▪ Random in-game events (e.g., "warehouse fire destroys 5 graded copies")
decrease supply.

Scarcity Multiplier=Initial Census−Locked CopiesInitial Census\text{Scarcity
Multiplier} = \frac{\text{Initial Census} - \text{Locked Copies}}{\text{Initial

What is Scarcity?

Scarcity means how rare something is. The fewer copies of a comic that are available, the more
valuable it becomes—like if you have the last cookie in a room full of hungry people, that cookie
is super important!

How Did We Measure Scarcity?
We started with how many total copies of each comic exist (Initial Census) and looked at how
many are locked away in people’s collections (Locked Supply). What’s left is how many are
still out there for trading.
•

What’s the Scarcity Multiplier?
The scarcity multiplier is a way of showing how much rarer a comic gets as more people lock it
away. If fewer are available, the multiplier goes higher.
•

60 available copies ÷ 100 total copies = 0.6 scarcity multiplier.

How Does Scarcity Change Price?
The scarcer something is, the higher the price goes! We use this formula:
New Price=Base Price×(1+Scarcity Multiplier)\text{New Price} = \text{Base Price} \times (1 +
\text{Scarcity Multiplier})New Price=Base Price×(1+Scarcity Multiplier)
•

o Scarcity multiplier: 0.6
o New price: $1,500,000 × (1 + 0.6) = $2,400,000

"hash": api_credentials["hash"]
}

def save_characters_to_db(conn, characters):
"""Save fetched Marvel characters into the database."""
cursor = conn.cursor()
for character in characters:
try:
cursor.execute("""
INSERT INTO marvel_characters (id, name, description,
thumbnail)
VALUES (%s, %s, %s, %s)
ON CONFLICT (id) DO NOTHING
""", (
character["id"],
character["name"],
character["description"],
f"{character['thumbnail']['path']}.{character['thumbnail']['extension']}"
))
except Exception as e:
print(f"Error saving character {character['name']}: {e}")
conn.commit()
cursor.close()
print(f"Inserted {len(characters)} characters into the database.")

Scarcity Multiplier
Assumptions:
- Scarcity Multiplier derived from census data as of 12/1/24.
- Sentiment Adjustment influenced by news and storyline
popularity.

2. Scarcity Equation:
o Scarcity Multiplier = 1 / (Census Count as of 12/1/24)
o Weighting adjustments can account for graded copies (e.g., CGC 9.8 vs. raw
issues).
3. Pricing at Lock-In:
o Use 12/1/24 as the fixed baseline for pricing:
▪ Market prices derived from sources like GPA Analysis, Go Collect, and
eBay sold listings.
o Adjust historical data to account for real-time changes leading to the 12/1/24 lockin.
4. Future Adjustments:
o Allow for price shifts based on sentiment and market factors, but the baseline
remains tied to 12/1/24.

# Save superhero data to database
def save_to_database(conn, data, source):
with conn.cursor() as cursor:
for item in data:
name = item.get("name", "Unknown")
description = item.get("description", "No description")
thumbnail = (
f"{item['thumbnail']['path']}.{item['thumbnail']['extension']}"
if "thumbnail" in item
else None
)
additional_data = str(item)
cursor.execute(
"""
INSERT INTO superhero_data (name, description, thumbnail,
source, additional_data)
VALUES (%s, %s, %s, %s, %s)
""",
(name, description, thumbnail, source, additional_data),
)
conn.commit()
print(f"Data from {source} saved successfully.")

def generate_easter_egg_idea(prompt="What is a fun Easter egg idea
for a trading game?"):
response = openai.ChatCompletion.create(
model="gpt-3.5-turbo",
messages=[
{"role": "system", "content": "You are a helpful assistant."},
{"role": "user", "content": prompt},
],
max_tokens=50
)
return response.choices[0].message["content"]

path
def fetch_data_from_database():
conn = sqlite3.connect(DATABASE_FILE)
cursor = conn×cursor()
cursor.execute("SELECT * FROM news;")
data = cursor.fetchall()
conn.close()
return data

MARVEL_API_URL = "https://gateway.marvel.com/v1/public/characters"
SUPERHERO_API_URL = "https://superheroapi.com/api/
{}/".format(SUPERHERO_API_KEY)
def fetch_marvel_character(name: str) -> Dict:
"""
Fetch character details from the Marvel API.
"""
params = {
"apikey": MARVEL_PUBLIC_KEY,
"ts": 1,
"hash": "YOUR_GENERATED_HASH", # Use MD5 hash of
ts+privateKey+publicKey
"name": name
}
response = requests.get(MARVEL_API_URL, params=params)
if response.status_code == 200:
return response.json()["data"]["results"]
else:
print(f"Marvel API error: {response.json()}")
return {}
def fetch_superhero_stats(id: int) -> Dict:
"""
Fetch character stats from Superhero API.
"""
response = requests.get(f"{SUPERHERO_API_URL}/{id}")
if response.status_code == 200:
return response.json()
else:
print(f"Superhero API error: {response.json()}")
return {}

def create_hash(ts, private_key, public_key):
"""Generate MD5 hash for the API request."""
return hashlib.md5(f"{ts}{private_key}
{public_key}".encode()).hexdigest()

2. Using the .env File in Your Code
To read environment variables from the .env file in your Python script,
you can use the python-dotenv library.

secret_key = os.getenv("SECRET_KEY")
print(f"API Key: {api_key}")

Thematic Integration into the Simulation
The essays can incorporate these truths to add richness to the financial
game:

who wasn’t relegated to a supporting role. He envisioned a character
who faced the same problems as Marvel’s readers—bullies at school,
money troubles, and social awkwardness—alongside his superheroic
adventures.
Lee pitched the idea of Spider-Man to his publisher, Martin Goodman,
who dismissed it as a terrible idea. Teenagers as leads? Too niche. A
hero with the powers of a spider? Gross. Undeterred, Lee slipped the
character into the final issue of the low-selling Amazing Fantasy series,
assuming it would quietly fade into obscurity. Instead, the story’s mix of
action, humor, and emotional depth struck a chord with readers,
catapulting Spider-Man to stardom.
Ditko’s Vision and Spider-Man’s Humanity
Steve Ditko’s influence on Spider-Man cannot be overstated. While Lee
provided the concept, Ditko gave the character his soul. His art
captured Peter Parker’s lanky awkwardness, his deep self-doubt, and
the oppressive weight of his responsibilities. Spider-Man wasn’t
glamorous or godlike—he was scrappy, clumsy, and often unsure of
himself.
Ditko’s meticulous, moody artwork also shaped the world of SpiderMan. From the shadowy alleys of New York City to the expressive detail
in Peter’s anguished face, Ditko grounded the story in realism, making
the character’s struggles feel immediate and relatable. The debut story
set the tone: after failing to stop a petty thief, Peter learns that the same
criminal has killed his beloved Uncle Ben. Wracked with guilt, Peter
embraces the mantra, “With great power comes great responsibility.” It’s
a line that resonates as powerfully today as it did in 1962.
Cultural Impact
Amazing Fantasy #15 turned the superhero genre on its head. Before
Spider-Man, heroes were often larger-than-life figures whose
challenges rarely mirrored those of ordinary readers. Spider-Man
changed that. He struggled with everything from rent payments to
romantic relationships, embodying the idea that being a hero doesn’t
make life easier—it often makes it harder.
Spider-Man also reflected the cultural zeitgeist of the 1960s. As the Civil
Rights Movement and counterculture gained momentum, Peter Parker

DC reviving horror anthologies like House of Secrets. It was in House of
Secrets #92 (1971) that Swamp Thing first appeared, created by Len
Wein and Bernie Wrightson as a one-off story.
The response to the character was so strong that DC launched an
ongoing series. Swamp Thing became a symbol of the era’s anxieties—
an avatar of nature fighting back against human exploitation and a
poignant commentary on the alienation of “the other.”
Bernie Wrightson’s Vision
Bernie Wrightson’s contribution to Swamp Thing cannot be overstated.
A master of horror illustration, Wrightson brought a level of detail and
atmosphere rarely seen in mainstream comics. His Swamp Thing was
more than a hulking monster; it was a creature of the earth, with
tendrils, roots, and vines entwined in every panel. Wrightson’s gothic
influences—from Edgar Allan Poe to Frankenstein—infused the series
with a haunting beauty.
In Swamp Thing #1, Alec Holland’s tragic transformation into the titular
creature is rendered with heartbreaking clarity. Wrightson’s ability to
depict both the grotesque and the sublime made Swamp Thing a visual
masterpiece. His artwork didn’t just tell the story—it set the mood,
immersing readers in the eerie, decaying swamps that mirrored Alec’s
own loss of humanity.
Thematic Depth
Beneath the horror, Swamp Thing explored profound themes of identity,
loss, and environmentalism. Alec Holland’s transformation into Swamp
Thing stripped him of his humanity, yet his sense of morality and love for
his wife, Linda, remained. This duality—being both man and monster—
created a character that was deeply empathetic despite its inhuman
appearance.

early years. By 1970, the series had been relegated to reprints, its future
uncertain.
The Claremont and Byrne Revolution
Everything changed in 1975 with Giant-Size X-Men #1. Written by Len
Wein and drawn by Dave Cockrum, this issue introduced a new, diverse
roster of mutants, including Wolverine, Storm, Colossus, and
Nightcrawler. Shortly after, Chris Claremont took over as writer, joined
by artist John Byrne, and together they transformed the X-Men into
Marvel’s flagship franchise.
Claremont’s writing brought a new level of complexity to the X-Men,
focusing on character development, interpersonal relationships, and
long-form storytelling. Byrne’s art gave the series a cinematic quality,
with dynamic layouts and expressive characters. Their collaboration
produced some of the most iconic X-Men stories, including the Dark
Phoenix Saga and Days of Future Past.
Under Claremont and Byrne, the X-Men became a metaphor for a wider
range of social issues, from LGBTQ+ rights to immigration. The mutants
weren’t just heroes—they were symbols of anyone marginalized by
society, giving readers a deeply personal connection to their struggles.

comic to focus exclusively on true crime stories. Published by Lev
Gleason and overseen by editor Charles Biro, the series promised
unflinching accounts of real-life criminals, complete with bloody crimes,
tragic downfalls, and a thin veneer of moral lessons.
Issue #24 marked a turning point for the series, introducing Mr. Crime,

hiding in secret lairs; it was lurking in alleyways and backrooms, waiting
to strike.

def resolve_conflicts(self, bot_name_1: str, bot_name_2: str):
"""Resolves task conflicts between two bots."""
logging.info(f"Conflict detected between {bot_name_1} and
{bot_name_2}")
# Simple resolution strategy: prioritize based on alphabetical order
winner = min(bot_name_1, bot_name_2)
logging.info(f"Conflict resolved: {winner} proceeds")
return winner

def resolve_conflicts(self, bot_name_1: str, bot_name_2: str):
"""Resolves task conflicts between two bots."""
logging.info(f"Conflict detected between {bot_name_1} and
{bot_name_2}")
winner = min(bot_name_1, bot_name_2)
logging.info(f"Conflict resolved: {winner} proceeds")
return winner

import logging
from taskrunnerbot import TaskRunnerBot # Import TaskRunnerBot
from typing import Callable

Central Orchestration Bot manages triggers and delegates tasks to
TaskRunnerBot.
"""
def __init__(self):
self.task_runner = TaskRunnerBot() # TaskRunnerBot integration
self.triggers = {} # Stores trigger callbacks
logging×basicConfig(level=logging.INFO, format="%(asctime)s [%
(levelname)s] %(message)s")
def register_trigger(self, trigger_name: str, callback: Callable):
"""
Registers a trigger and its callback.
"""
self.triggers[trigger_name] = callback
logging.info(f"Trigger registered: {trigger_name}")
def handle_trigger(self, trigger_name: str):
"""
Activates a trigger and registers a task with TaskRunnerBot.
"""

Delegates task execution to TaskRunnerBot.
"""
self.task_runner.run_tasks()

TaskRunnerBot as a task.
. Task Execution:

success to the orchestrator.

BASE_URL = "https://gateway.marvel.com/v1/public"
def generate_hash(ts, private_key, public_key):
"""Generate MD5 hash required for Marvel API authentication."""
to_hash = f"{ts}{private_key}{public_key}"
return md5(to_hash.encode()).hexdigest()

The error indicates an SSL issue during the connection to Marvel's API.
This is likely related to an SSL/TLS misconfiguration. Here's how to
resolve it step by step:
Steps to Fix
1. Upgrade requests and certifi
Ensure you’re using the latest versions of requests and certifi:
bash
Copy code
pip install --upgrade requests certifi
2. Verify OpenSSL Configuration
Check the OpenSSL version being used by Python:
bash
Copy code
python -m ssl
You should see something like this:
plaintext
Copy code
OpenSSL 1.1.1 or higher
If it’s older or missing, update OpenSSL:
bash
Copy code
brew install openssl
brew link openssl --force
3. Disable SSL Verification (Debug Only)
Temporarily disable SSL verification to confirm the issue:
python
Copy code

curl -X GET "https://gateway.marvel.com/v1/public/comics?
apikey=b13bf02cdd452c74e00544a0f8ed1165&ts=1&hash=7f0d3711c0
0800659b2aafe13c336f13&titleStartsWith=hulk&limit=10"
If it works, the issue is with your Python environment.
5. Use a Proxy
Sometimes corporate firewalls or network configurations cause SSL
errors. Test with a proxy:
python
Copy code
proxies = {
"http": "http://proxy.example.com:8080",
"https": "http://proxy.example.com:8080",
}
response = requests.get(MARVEL_API_URL, params=params,
proxies=proxies)
6. Add Retry Logic
Add retry logic to handle intermittent SSL errors:
python
Copy code
from requests.adapters import HTTPAdapter
from requests.packages.urllib3.util.retry import Retry
session = requests×Session()
retries = Retry(total=5, backoff_factor=0.3, status_forcelist=[500, 502,
503, 504])
session.mount("https://", HTTPAdapter(max_retries=retries))
response = session.get(MARVEL_API_URL, params=params)

environments on the planet. Found almost exclusively in the Southern
Hemisphere, penguins are flightless birds that have adapted remarkably
to a life of swimming rather than flying. This essay explores the unique
physical adaptations, behaviors, and ecological significance of
penguins.
The Physical Marvels of Penguins
Penguins’ bodies are a testament to evolutionary ingenuity. Unlike most
birds, penguins are flightless, but this is not a disadvantage. Their wings
have evolved into powerful flippers, making them exceptional swimmers.
With their streamlined bodies, penguins can reach speeds of up to 15
miles per hour underwater, using their flippers to "fly" through the water
with remarkable agility. Their dense bones reduce buoyancy, allowing
them to dive to great depths in pursuit of food, such as fish, krill, and
squid.
Another critical adaptation is their feathers. Penguins boast a thick layer
of waterproof feathers that overlap like shingles, providing insulation
against the frigid waters of their Antarctic and sub-Antarctic habitats.
Beneath this feathered layer lies a thick deposit of blubber, which
further insulates them from the cold. Their black-and-white coloration is
not just charming but serves as effective camouflage. Known as
counter-shading, their dark backs blend with the ocean depths when

often exclusive to Pro users.
Region Restrictions: Some features are rolled out in phases or
limited to specific regions.
Contact Support: Use the feedback or support options in
ChatGPT.

. Partner APIs: Exclusively shared with specific business
partners, these APIs facilitate collaboration and data sharing
between partnered organizations.
. Internal APIs (Private APIs): Designed for internal use within
an organization, they streamline operations across different
teams and systems.
. Composite APIs: These combine multiple service and data
APIs, allowing developers to access several endpoints in a single
call, enhancing efficiency.
Common Architectures:
●
